     <p>
      <i>
       <b>
        What a Cartoon!
       </b>
      </i>
      (later known as
      <i>
       <b>
        The What a Cartoon! Show
       </b>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <b>
        The Cartoon Cartoon Show
       </b>
      </i>
      ) is an American
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_animated_television_series" title="List of animated television series">
       animation showcase
      </a>
      series created by
      <a href="/wiki/Fred_Seibert" title="Fred Seibert">
       Fred Seibert
      </a>
      for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanna-Barbera_Productions" title="Hanna-Barbera Productions">
       Hanna-Barbera Productions
      </a>
      which aired on
      <a href="/wiki/Cartoon_Network" title="Cartoon Network">
       Cartoon Network
      </a>
      . The project consisted of 82 short cartoons, intended to return creative power to animators and artists, by recreating the atmospheres that spawned the iconic cartoon characters of the mid-20th century. Each of the shorts mirrored the structure of a theatrical cartoon, with each film being based on an original storyboard drawn and written by its artist or creator.
     </p>
     <p>
      The series first aired on February 20, 1995, and the shorts were promoted as
      <i>
       <b>
        World Premiere Toons
       </b>
      </i>
      . During the original run of the shorts, the series was retitled to
      <i>
       The What a Cartoon! Show
      </i>
      and later to
      <i>
       The Cartoon Cartoon Show
      </i>
      until the final short aired on August 23, 2002. The project served as the launching point for multiple Cartoon Network series, including
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dexter%27s_Laboratory" title="Dexter's Laboratory">
        Dexter's Laboratory
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Bravo" title="Johnny Bravo">
        Johnny Bravo
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Cow_and_Chicken" title="Cow and Chicken">
        Cow and Chicken
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Powerpuff_Girls" title="The Powerpuff Girls">
        The Powerpuff Girls
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Courage_the_Cowardly_Dog" title="Courage the Cowardly Dog">
        Courage the Cowardly Dog
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mike,_Lu_%26_Og" title="Mike, Lu &amp; Og">
        Mike, Lu &amp; Og
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sheep_in_the_Big_City" title="Sheep in the Big City">
        Sheep in the Big City
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Whatever_Happened_to..._Robot_Jones%3F" title="Whatever Happened to... Robot Jones?">
        Whatever Happened to... Robot Jones?
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Codename:_Kids_Next_Door" title="Codename: Kids Next Door">
        Codename: Kids Next Door
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Grim_Adventures_of_Billy_%26_Mandy" title="The Grim Adventures of Billy &amp; Mandy">
        The Grim Adventures of Billy &amp; Mandy
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Megas_XLR" title="Megas XLR">
        Megas XLR
       </a>
      </i>
      , as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Fox_Broadcasting_Company" title="Fox Broadcasting Company">
       Fox
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Family_Guy" title="Family Guy">
        Family Guy
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The series is influential for birthing a slew of original Cartoon Network hits and helping to revive television animation in the 1990s. Once it had several original shorts, those became the first Cartoon Cartoons (a collective term for retro Cartoon Network original series). In its final years,
      <i>
       The Cartoon Cartoon Show
      </i>
      became a block for reruns of older Cartoon Cartoons that had been phased out by the network.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fred_Seibert_by_Gage_Skidmore.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         What a Cartoon!
        </i>
        creator
        <a href="/wiki/Fred_Seibert" title="Fred Seibert">
         Fred Seibert
        </a>
        at
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vidcon" title="Vidcon">
         Vidcon
        </a>
        2014.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Fred_Seibert" title="Fred Seibert">
       Fred Seibert
      </a>
      became president of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hanna-Barbera_Cartoons" title="Hanna-Barbera Cartoons">
       Hanna-Barbera Cartoons
      </a>
      in 1992 and helped guide the struggling animation studio into its greatest output in years with shows like
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/2_Stupid_Dogs" title="2 Stupid Dogs">
        2 Stupid Dogs
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/SWAT_Kats:_The_Radical_Squadron" title="SWAT Kats: The Radical Squadron">
        SWAT Kats: The Radical Squadron
       </a>
      </i>
      . Seibert wanted the studio to produce short cartoons, in the vein of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_American_animation" title="Golden Age of American animation">
       Golden Age of American animation
      </a>
      . Although a project consisting of 48 shorts would cost twice as much as a normal series,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part15_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-part15-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Seibert's pitch to Cartoon Network involved promising 48 chances to "succeed or fail", opened up possibilities for new original programming, and offered several new shorts to the thousands already present in the
      <a href="/wiki/Turner_Entertainment" title="Turner Entertainment">
       Turner Entertainment
      </a>
      library. According to Seibert, quality did not matter much to the cable operators distributing the struggling network, they were more interested in promising new programs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part17_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-part17-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      With Turner Broadcasting CEO
      <a href="/wiki/Ted_Turner" title="Ted Turner">
       Ted Turner
      </a>
      and Seibert's boss
      <a href="/wiki/Scott_Sassa" title="Scott Sassa">
       Scott Sassa
      </a>
      on board, the studio fanned out across the world to spread the word that the studio was in an "unprecedented phase", in which animators had a better idea what cartoons should be than executives and Hanna-Barbera supported them.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part20_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-part20-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part22_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-part22-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The company starting taking pitches in earnest in 1993 and received over 5,000 pitches for the 48 slots. The diversity in the filmmakers included those from various nationalities, race, and gender. Seibert later described his hope for an idealistic diversity as "The wider the palette of creative influences, the wider and bigger the audiences."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part22_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-part22-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Seibert's idea for the project was influenced heavily by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Looney_Tunes" title="Looney Tunes">
        Looney Tunes
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part22_4-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-part22-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hanna-Barbera founders and chairmen
      <a href="/wiki/William_Hanna" title="William Hanna">
       William Hanna
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Barbera" title="Joseph Barbera">
       Joseph Barbera
      </a>
      , as well as veteran animator
      <a href="/wiki/Friz_Freleng" title="Friz Freleng">
       Friz Freleng
      </a>
      , taught Seibert how the shorts of the Golden Age of American animation were produced.
      <a href="/wiki/John_Kricfalusi" title="John Kricfalusi">
       John Kricfalusi
      </a>
      , creator of
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Ren_and_Stimpy_Show" title="The Ren and Stimpy Show">
        The Ren and Stimpy Show
       </a>
      </i>
      , became a teacher of sorts for Seibert and was the first person Seibert called while looking for new talent for the project.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As was the custom in live action film and television, the company did not pay each creator for the storyboard submitted and pitched. For the first time in the studio's history, individual creators could retain their rights, and earn royalties on their creations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN_5-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While most in the industry scoffed at the idea, encouragement, according to Seibert, came from the cartoonists who flocked to Hanna-Barbera with original ideas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part18_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-part18-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Format">
       Format
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=What_a_Cartoon!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Format">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The format for
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      was ambitious, as no one had ever attempted anything similar in the
      <a href="/wiki/Animation_in_the_United_States_in_the_television_era" title="Animation in the United States in the television era">
       television animation era
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part22_4-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-part22-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The shorts produced would be a product of the original cartoonists' vision, with no executive intervention: for example, even the music would be an individually crafted score. Each "
      <i>
       Looney Tunes
      </i>
      length" (7 minutes) short would debut, by itself, as a stand-alone cartoon on Cartoon Network.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part20_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-part20-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part22_4-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-part22-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Seibert explained the project's goal in a 2007 blog post: "We didnt care what the
      <a href="/wiki/Sitcom" title="Sitcom">
       sitcom
      </a>
      trends were, what
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickelodeon_(TV_channel)" title="Nickelodeon (TV channel)">
       Nickelodeon
      </a>
      was doing, what the sales departments wanted. [...] We wanted cartoons."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-part22_4-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-part22-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Crew">
       Crew
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=What_a_Cartoon!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Crew">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <table class="toccolours" style="float: right; margin-left: 1em; margin-right: 2em; font-size: 85%; background:#c6dbf7; color:black; width:30em; max-width: 40%;">
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align: left;">
        "On top of [a research and development program], I reinvigorated the 'who comes in the studio' equation. Now talented people wanted to show up. Some 5,000 people pitched us cartoons from all over the world. We got into business with
        <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Bakshi" title="Ralph Bakshi">
         Ralph Bakshi
        </a>
        , with
        <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Bozzetto" title="Bruno Bozzetto">
         Bruno Bozzetto
        </a>
        ; we got into business with a broad range of people who never would've given Hanna-Barbera a passing chance. We worked with people who were 70 years old, who were 20 years old. We turned on its head the perception the people in the community had of us."
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align: left;">
         Creator
        <a href="/wiki/Fred_Seibert" title="Fred Seibert">
         Fred Seibert
        </a>
        on the variety of directors for
        <i>
         What a Cartoon!
        </i>
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN_5-2">
         <a href="#cite_note-AWN-5">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          5
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      staff had creators from Europe and Canada (
      <a href="/wiki/Bruno_Bozzetto" title="Bruno Bozzetto">
       Bruno Bozzetto
      </a>
      ), Asia (Achiu So), and the United States (Jerry Reynolds and colleague
      <a href="/wiki/Seth_MacFarlane" title="Seth MacFarlane">
       Seth MacFarlane
      </a>
      ). The crew also contained young series first timers (like
      <a href="/wiki/Genndy_Tartakovsky" title="Genndy Tartakovsky">
       Genndy Tartakovsky
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Craig_McCracken" title="Craig McCracken">
       Craig McCracken
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rob_Renzetti" title="Rob Renzetti">
       Rob Renzetti
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Butch_Hartman" title="Butch Hartman">
       Butch Hartman
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Dilworth" title="John Dilworth">
       John Dilworth
      </a>
      ), but veterans as well (like Don Jurwich,
      <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Eisenberg" title="Jerry Eisenberg">
       Jerry Eisenberg
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Bakshi" title="Ralph Bakshi">
       Ralph Bakshi
      </a>
      ). In addition to the veterans,
      <a href="/wiki/William_Hanna" title="William Hanna">
       William Hanna
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Barbera" title="Joseph Barbera">
       Joseph Barbera
      </a>
      each produced two shorts each for
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      . Many of the key crew members from previous Hanna-Barbera series
      <i>
       2 Stupid Dogs
      </i>
      joined the team of
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      as well.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN_5-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many of the crew members of
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      later went on to write and direct for
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dexter%27s_Laboratory" title="Dexter's Laboratory">
        Dexter's Laboratory
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Bravo" title="Johnny Bravo">
        Johnny Bravo
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Cow_and_Chicken" title="Cow and Chicken">
        Cow and Chicken
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/I_Am_Weasel" title="I Am Weasel">
        I Am Weasel
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Powerpuff_Girls" title="The Powerpuff Girls">
        The Powerpuff Girls
       </a>
      </i>
      , including those named above.
      <i>
       The Kitchen Casanova
      </i>
      director John McIntyre is particularly known for directing several
      <i>
       Dexter
      </i>
      episodes. Ralph Bakshi's two shorts (
      <i>
       Malcom and Melvin
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Babe! He... Calls Me
      </i>
      ) were considered too risqu to be shown.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN6_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN6-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been rumored that
      <a href="/wiki/John_Kricfalusi" title="John Kricfalusi">
       John Kricfalusi
      </a>
      was slated to direct several new
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      shorts of his own (produced by his production company,
      <a href="/wiki/Sp%C3%BCmc%C3%B8" title="Spmc">
       Spmc
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN4_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN4-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, both
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Yogi_Bear" title="Yogi Bear">
        Yogi Bear
       </a>
      </i>
      -influenced cartoons were commissioned separately by Seibert, and instead premiered as their own:
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Boo_Boo_Runs_Wild" title="Boo Boo Runs Wild">
        Boo Boo Runs Wild
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/A_Day_in_the_Life_of_Ranger_Smith" title="A Day in the Life of Ranger Smith">
        A Day in the Life of Ranger Smith
       </a>
      </i>
      both premiered in 1999.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN5_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN5-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Inspired by Seibert's interest in the modern rock posters of
      <a href="/wiki/Frank_Kozik" title="Frank Kozik">
       Frank Kozik
      </a>
      , each of the shows' creators worked with the internal Hanna-Barbera Creative Corps Creative Director Bill Burnett, and Senior Art Director
      <a href="/wiki/Jesse_Stagg" title="Jesse Stagg">
       Jesse Stagg
      </a>
      to craft a series of high quality, limited edition, fluorescent art posters. The Corps launched a prolonged Guerrilla mailing campaign, targeting animation heavyweights and critics leading up to the launch of
      <i>
       World Premiere Toons
      </i>
      . The first poster campaign of its kind introduced the world to the groundbreaking new stable of characters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Broadcast">
       Broadcast
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=What_a_Cartoon!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Broadcast">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WorldPremiereToons.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="1050" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/WorldPremiereToons.png/220px-WorldPremiereToons.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/WorldPremiereToons.png/330px-WorldPremiereToons.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/67/WorldPremiereToons.png/440px-WorldPremiereToons.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WorldPremiereToons.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Intertitle for
        <i>
         What a Cartoon
        </i>
        's
        <i>
         World Premiere Toons
        </i>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The first cartoon from the
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      project broadcast in its entirety was
      <i>
       The Powerpuff Girls in Meat Fuzzy Lumkins
      </i>
      , which made its world premiere on Monday, February 20, 1995, during a television special called the
      <i>
       World Premiere Toon-In
      </i>
      (termed "President's Day Nightmare" by its producers,
      <a href="/wiki/Williams_Street" title="Williams Street">
       Williams Street
      </a>
      ). The special was hosted by
      <a href="/wiki/Space_Ghost" title="Space Ghost">
       Space Ghost
      </a>
      and the cast of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Space_Ghost_Coast_to_Coast" title="Space Ghost Coast to Coast">
        Space Ghost Coast to Coast
       </a>
      </i>
      , and featured comic interviews and a mock contest with the creators of the various cartoons. The Toon-In was simulcast on Cartoon Network,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TBS_Superstation" title="TBS Superstation">
       TBS Superstation
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/TNT_(TV_channel)" title="TNT (TV channel)">
       TNT
      </a>
      . To promote the shorts, Cartoon Network's marketing department came up with the concept of "Dive-In Theater" in 1995 to showcase the 48 cartoon shorts. The cartoons were shown at water parks and large municipal swimming pools, treating kids and their parents to exclusive poolside screenings on 9' x 12' movie screens.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN3_11-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN3-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Beginning February 26, 1995, each
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      short began to premiere on Sunday nights, promoted as
      <i>
       World Premiere Toons
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dailynews_12-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dailynews-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Every week after the premiere, Cartoon Network showcased a different World Premiere Toons made by a different artist. After an acclimation of cartoons, the network packaged the shorts as a half-hour show titled
      <i>
       World Premiere Toons: The Next Generation
      </i>
      , featuring reruns of the original shorts but also new premieres.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TWAC.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="320" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/63/TWAC.jpg/220px-TWAC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/63/TWAC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/63/TWAC.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TWAC.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Intertitle for
        <i>
         What a Cartoon!
        </i>
        in its second incarnation,
        <i>
         The What a Cartoon! Show
        </i>
        <small style="font-size:85%;">
         (199697)
        </small>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Eventually, all of the cartoons were compiled into one program bearing the name of the original project:
      <i>
       The What a Cartoon! Show
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN_5-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The show's initial premieres for each short preceded Cartoon Network's Sunday night movie block,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_programs_broadcast_by_Cartoon_Network" title="List of programs broadcast by Cartoon Network">
        Mr. Spim's Cartoon Theatre
       </a>
      </i>
      . The shorts continued to air on Sundays until 1997, when the network moved the shorts to Wednesdays at 9pm.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SLATE_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SLATE-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the premiere of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Bravo" title="Johnny Bravo">
        Johnny Bravo
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Cow_and_Chicken" title="Cow and Chicken">
        Cow and Chicken
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/I_Am_Weasel" title="I Am Weasel">
        I Am Weasel
       </a>
      </i>
      as full series in July 1997, the series shifted to Thursday nights, where it remained.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eagle_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-eagle-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <i>
       The What a Cartoon! Show
      </i>
      continued airing new episodes on Thursdays until November 28, 1997, when the final short of the 48 contracted during Seibert's era aired. In 1998, Cartoon Network debuted two new short pilots and advertised them as
      <i>
       World Premiere Toons
      </i>
      :
      <i>
       Mike, Lu &amp; Og
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Kenny and the Chimp in "Diseasy Does It!"
      </i>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN4_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN4-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      both of which were produced by outside studios. The two pilots were later compiled into
      <i>
       The Cartoon Cartoon Show
      </i>
      , while both shorts eventually garnered their own series,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mike,_Lu_%26_Og" title="Mike, Lu &amp; Og">
        Mike, Lu &amp; Og
       </a>
      </i>
      in 1999 and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Codename:_Kids_Next_Door" title="Codename: Kids Next Door">
        Codename: Kids Next Door
       </a>
      </i>
      in 2002. Two pilots entitled
      <i>
       King Crab: Space Crustacean
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Thrillseeker
      </i>
      , respectively dated 1999 and 2000, was also retconned into
      <i>
       The Cartoon Cartoon Show
      </i>
      anthology.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TCCS2000.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1030" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/TCCS2000.png/220px-TCCS2000.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/TCCS2000.png/330px-TCCS2000.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8e/TCCS2000.png/440px-TCCS2000.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TCCS2000.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Intertitle for
        <i>
         What a Cartoon!
        </i>
        in its third incarnation,
        <i>
         The Cartoon Cartoon Show
        </i>
        <small style="font-size:85%;">
         (200003)
        </small>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On June 9, 2000,
      <i>
       The What a Cartoon! Show
      </i>
      was relaunched as
      <i>
       The Cartoon Cartoon Show
      </i>
      . In this new format, it aired reruns and new episodes of the full-series
      <a href="/wiki/Cartoon_Cartoons" title="Cartoon Cartoons">
       Cartoon Cartoons
      </a>
      , as well as new Cartoon Cartoon shorts and old
      <i>
       WAC!
      </i>
      shorts. From 2000 to 2001, the pilot shorts appearing on the network's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cartoon_Network%27s_Big_Pick" title="Cartoon Network's Big Pick">
       viewer's poll
      </a>
      that lost to
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Grim_Adventures_of_Billy_%26_Mandy" title="The Grim Adventures of Billy &amp; Mandy">
        The Grim Adventures of Billy &amp; Mandy
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Codename:_Kids_Next_Door" title="Codename: Kids Next Door">
        Codename: Kids Next Door
       </a>
      </i>
      were added to the anthology. The show continued to air until October 9, 2003, when it was temporarily dropped from the network's schedule.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TheCartoonCartoonShow.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="960" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/TheCartoonCartoonShow.jpg/220px-TheCartoonCartoonShow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/TheCartoonCartoonShow.jpg/330px-TheCartoonCartoonShow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/04/TheCartoonCartoonShow.jpg/440px-TheCartoonCartoonShow.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TheCartoonCartoonShow.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Intertitle for
        <i>
         The Cartoon Cartoon Show
        </i>
        in its revived form
        <small style="font-size:85%;">
         (200508)
        </small>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On September 12, 2005,
      <i>
       The Cartoon Cartoon Show
      </i>
      was revived, this time as a half-hour program featuring segments of older Cartoon Cartoons that were no longer shown regularly on the network, such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Bravo" title="Johnny Bravo">
        Johnny Bravo
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Powerpuff_Girls" title="The Powerpuff Girls">
        The Powerpuff Girls
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/I_Am_Weasel" title="I Am Weasel">
        I Am Weasel
       </a>
      </i>
      , and others. Some Cartoon Cartoons were moved exclusively to this show and the Top 5, though there was also some overlap with shows that already had regular half-hour slots outside the series (such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Courage_the_Cowardly_Dog" title="Courage the Cowardly Dog">
        Courage the Cowardly Dog
       </a>
      </i>
      , which returned in October 2006 as part of The Grim and Courage Hour). In 2006, the programming was expanded to also include non-Cartoon Cartoons that
      <i>
       were
      </i>
      regularly shown on the network, such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Foster%27s_Home_for_Imaginary_Friends" title="Foster's Home for Imaginary Friends">
        Foster's Home for Imaginary Friends
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Camp_Lazlo" title="Camp Lazlo">
        Camp Lazlo
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/My_Gym_Partner%27s_a_Monkey" title="My Gym Partner's a Monkey">
        My Gym Partner's a Monkey
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Squirrel_Boy" title="Squirrel Boy">
        Squirrel Boy
       </a>
      </i>
      . The show ended on June 1, 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2007, reruns of
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      played briefly on Cartoon Network's retro animation sister channel,
      <a href="/wiki/Boomerang_(TV_channel)" title="Boomerang (TV channel)">
       Boomerang
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-schedule_20070603_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-schedule_20070603-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">
       Legacy
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=What_a_Cartoon!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Legacy">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dexter%27s_Laboratory" title="Dexter's Laboratory">
        Dexter's Laboratory
       </a>
      </i>
      was the most popular short series according to a vote held in 1995 and eventually became the first
      <a href="/wiki/Spin-off_(media)" title="Spin-off (media)">
       spin-off
      </a>
      of
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      in 1996. Two more series based on shorts,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Johnny_Bravo" title="Johnny Bravo">
        Johnny Bravo
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Cow_and_Chicken" title="Cow and Chicken">
        Cow and Chicken
       </a>
      </i>
      , premiered in 1997, and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Powerpuff_Girls" title="The Powerpuff Girls">
        The Powerpuff Girls
       </a>
      </i>
      became a weekly half-hour show in 1998.
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Courage_the_Cowardly_Dog" title="Courage the Cowardly Dog">
        Courage the Cowardly Dog
       </a>
      </i>
      (spun off from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">
       Oscar
      </a>
      -nominated short
      <i>
       The Chicken from Outer Space
      </i>
      ) followed as the final spin-off in 1999. In addition, the
      <i>
       Cow and Chicken
      </i>
      short
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/I_Am_Weasel" title="I Am Weasel">
        I Am Weasel
       </a>
      </i>
      eventually was also spun off into a separate series: in all, six cartoon series were ultimately launched by the
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      project, any one of which earned enough money for the company to pay for the whole program.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN_5-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In addition to the eventual spin-offs, the
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      short
      <i>
       Larry and Steve
      </i>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Seth_MacFarlane" title="Seth MacFarlane">
       Seth MacFarlane
      </a>
      featured prototypes of characters that would later go on to become MacFarlane's massively successful
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Family_Guy" title="Family Guy">
        Family Guy
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      project and its assorted spin-offs brought Cartoon Network more commercial and critical success, and the network became an animation industry leader as the 1990s drew to a close. In 2001, coinciding with the death of William Hanna, Hanna-Barbera Productions merged with
      <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Animation" title="Warner Bros. Animation">
       Warner Bros. Animation
      </a>
      and Cartoon Network opened its own production arm,
      <a href="/wiki/Cartoon_Network_Studios" title="Cartoon Network Studios">
       Cartoon Network Studios
      </a>
      , in Burbank, as the rightful Hanna-Barbera successor to produce original programming for the network and future projects. Two
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      shorts,
      <i>
       Windup Wolf
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       Hard Luck Duck
      </i>
      , were the last cartoon shorts directed and produced by co-founder and co-chairman William Hanna. In addition,
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      and spin-offs were the final original productions released by Hanna-Barbera.
     </p>
     <p>
      Creator of the
      <i>
       What a Cartoon! Show
      </i>
      , Fred Seibert, left Hanna-Barbera in late 1996 to open up his own studio,
      <a href="/wiki/Frederator_Studios" title="Frederator Studios">
       Frederator Studios
      </a>
      , and has persistently continued in the tradition of surfacing new talent, characters, and series with similar shorts incubators, including (as of 2015)
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Oh_Yeah!_Cartoons" title="Oh Yeah! Cartoons">
        Oh Yeah! Cartoons
       </a>
      </i>
      (Nickelodeon, 1998),
      <i>
       The Meth Minute 39
      </i>
      (Channel Frederator, 2008),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Random!_Cartoons" title="Random! Cartoons">
        Random! Cartoons
       </a>
      </i>
      (Nickelodeon/Nicktoons, 2008),
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Too_Cool!_Cartoons" title="Too Cool! Cartoons">
        Too Cool! Cartoons
       </a>
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Cartoon_Hangover" title="Cartoon Hangover">
       Cartoon Hangover
      </a>
      , 2012), and
      <i>
       GO! Cartoons
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Cartoon_Hangover" title="Cartoon Hangover">
       Cartoon Hangover
      </a>
      , 2016).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Oh_Yeah!_Cartoons" title="Oh Yeah! Cartoons">
        Oh Yeah! Cartoons
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWN2_20-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AWN2-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      showcased
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      alumni (
      <a href="/wiki/Butch_Hartman" title="Butch Hartman">
       Butch Hartman
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Rob_Renzetti" title="Rob Renzetti">
       Rob Renzetti
      </a>
      ) and launched several successful Nickelodeon series, including
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Fairly_OddParents" title="The Fairly OddParents">
        The Fairly OddParents
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/ChalkZone" title="ChalkZone">
        ChalkZone
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/My_Life_as_a_Teenage_Robot" title="My Life as a Teenage Robot">
        My Life as a Teenage Robot
       </a>
      </i>
      . Frederator Studios launched another animation showcase in 2006, titled
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Random!_Cartoons" title="Random! Cartoons">
        Random! Cartoons
       </a>
      </i>
      , which in turn produced
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nickelodeon_(TV_channel)" title="Nickelodeon (TV channel)">
       Nickelodeon
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fanboy_and_Chum_Chum" title="Fanboy and Chum Chum">
        Fanboy and Chum Chum
       </a>
      </i>
      in 2009 and
      <a href="/wiki/Cartoon_Network" title="Cartoon Network">
       Cartoon Network
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Adventure_Time" title="Adventure Time">
        Adventure Time
       </a>
      </i>
      in 2010.
     </p>
     <p>
      A sequel-of-sorts to the
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      project, a Cartoon Network project titled
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Cartoonstitute" title="The Cartoonstitute">
        The Cartoonstitute
       </a>
      </i>
      , was announced in April 2008. Created by Cartoon Network executive Rob Sorcher and headed by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Powerpuff_Girls" title="The Powerpuff Girls">
        The Powerpuff Girls
       </a>
      </i>
      creator
      <a href="/wiki/Craig_McCracken" title="Craig McCracken">
       Craig McCracken
      </a>
      , the project was to "establish a think tank and create an environment in which animators can create characters and stories", and also create new possible Cartoon Network series.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ToonZone_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ToonZone-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AnimationInsider_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AnimationInsider-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the project was eventually scrapped as a result of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_2000s_recession" title="Late 2000s recession">
       late 2000s recession
      </a>
      and only 14 of the 39 planned were completed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-deviantArt_23-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-deviantArt-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nevertheless,
      <a href="/wiki/J._G._Quintel" title="J. G. Quintel">
       J. G. Quintel
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Regular_Show" title="Regular Show">
        Regular Show
       </a>
      </i>
      short and Pete Browngardt's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Secret_Mountain_Fort_Awesome" title="Secret Mountain Fort Awesome">
        Secret Mountain Fort Awesome
       </a>
      </i>
      were greenlit to become full series. A recurring character on the show, Uncle Grandpa, would get his
      <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Grandpa" title="Uncle Grandpa">
       own series
      </a>
      two years later.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MultiChannel_24-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MultiChannel-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Big_Cartoon_DataBase" title="Big Cartoon DataBase">
       Big Cartoon DataBase
      </a>
      cites
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      as a "venture combining classic 1940s production methods with the originality, enthusiasm and comedy of the 1990s".
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_shorts">
       List of shorts
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=What_a_Cartoon!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: List of shorts">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Original_show">
       Original show
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=What_a_Cartoon!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Original show">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The following is a list of the original shorts produced under
      <a href="/wiki/Fred_Seibert" title="Fred Seibert">
       Fred Seibert
      </a>
      's management for
      <i>
       What a Cartoon!
      </i>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Hanna-Barbera" title="Hanna-Barbera">
       Hanna-Barbera
      </a>
      . The shorts are listed in by director and/or creator, and do not contain extra cartoons added on by
      <a href="/wiki/Cartoon_Network" title="Cartoon Network">
       Cartoon Network
      </a>
      when the show was re-titled
      <i>
       The Cartoon Cartoon Show
      </i>
      .
     </p>

     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cartoon_Cartoon_shorts">
       Cartoon Cartoon shorts
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=What_a_Cartoon!&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cartoon Cartoon shorts">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Two Cartoon Cartoon shorts were produced in 1998 and one in 1999. All Cartoon Cartoon shorts produced between 2000 and 2001 were entered in
      <i>
       The Big Pick
      </i>
      , a contest to choose the newest Cartoon Cartoon. The shorts premiered on Cartoon Cartoon Fridays in the weeks leading up to "The Big Pick" and the winner was revealed during the actual event. The winners were
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Grim_Adventures_of_Billy_%26_Mandy" title="The Grim Adventures of Billy &amp; Mandy">
        The Grim Adventures of Billy &amp; Mandy
       </a>
      </i>
      , in 2000, and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Codename:_Kids_Next_Door" title="Codename: Kids Next Door">
        Codename: Kids Next Door
       </a>
      </i>
      , in 2001.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 2002, 8 new shorts premiered during the Cartoon Cartoon Weekend Summerfest, only this time, they weren't competing against one another. These were the final Cartoon Cartoon shorts before the brand name was dropped. One short,
      <i>
       LowBrow
      </i>
      , was given its own series called
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Megas_XLR" title="Megas XLR">
        Megas XLR
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>

       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text">
        <span class="mbox-text-span">
         This section requires
         <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=What_a_Cartoon!&amp;action=edit">
          expansion
         </a>
         .
         <small>
          <i>
           (November 2014)
          </i>
         </small>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      From 2000 to 2003,
      <i>
       The Cartoon Cartoon Show
      </i>
      featured new episodes and reruns of the full-series Cartoon Cartoons, interspersed with premieres and reruns of the Cartoon Cartoon shorts (some of which were retconned
      <i>
       WAC!
      </i>
      shorts). From 2005 to 2008, the block was revived, this time dropping the shorts. Episodes from each show were anthologized into 7 or 11-minute segments.
     </p>
     <p>
      This is a list of shows that were presented on the block:
     </p>
